  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d0d5fc50-3226-4e43-af7b-f4adeaa36629'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer8795193614691111722">The novel that launched the astonishing career of one of the 20th century’s greatest writers of action and suspense – an acclaimed classic of heroism and the sea in World War II. Now reissued in a new cover style.<p>The story of men who rose to heroism, and then to something greater, <i>HMS Ulysses</i> takes its place alongside <i>The Caine Mutiny</i> and <i>The Cruel Sea</i> as one of the classi</p></span>
  <span id="freeText8795193614691111722" style="display:none">The novel that launched the astonishing career of one of the 20th century’s greatest writers of action and suspense – an acclaimed classic of heroism and the sea in World War II. Now reissued in a new cover style.<p>The story of men who rose to heroism, and then to something greater, <i>HMS Ulysses</i> takes its place alongside <i>The Caine Mutiny</i> and <i>The Cruel Sea</i> as one of the classic novels of the navy at war.</p><p>It is the compelling story of Convoy FR77 to Murmansk – a voyage that pushes men to the limits of human endurance, crippled by enemy attack and the bitter cold of the Arctic.</p></span>

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1119408-what-does-far-turn-of-the-quarter-circle">What does &quot;far turn of the quarter circle&quot; mean?
And then another great sea, towering almost to bridge height, caught her on the far turn of the quarter circle. It struck the entire length of the Ulysses, for the first time that night, with tremendous weight.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1119408'><a id="like_count_community_question_1119408" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1119408?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F149667.HMS_Ulysses">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1119408-what-does-far-turn-of-the-quarter-circle">3 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1119408-what-does-far-turn-of-the-quarter-circle">See all 3 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/12237307-julian"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1345993145i/12237307._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/12237307-julian">Julian</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">If you imagine a ship ordered to sail in a circular direction (orbiting a point), the point where it starts to execute the order is "near", and the fu<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">If you imagine a ship ordered to sail in a circular direction (orbiting a point), the point where it starts to execute the order is "near", and the furthest point is "far". Given that it is on a moving sea, this requires the execution of multiple steering commands. The progress around the turn is measured in quarters. e.g. If the turn starts at the 6 o'Clock position, and sails clockwise towards the 12 o'clock position, then between 6 and 9 would be the near quarter, between 9 and 12 would be the far quarter<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1047638130" />
    <a title="Leila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15194189-leila"><img alt="Leila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397822707p2/15194189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1047638130?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leila" href="/user/show/15194189-leila">Leila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=my-wow-books">my-wow-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=my-challenge-books">my-challenge-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=sea-stories">sea-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=so-many-books-so-little-time">so-many-books-so-little-time</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15194189-leila?shelf=war">war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1047638130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12031294983686450618">I first read this brilliant heart-stopping book years ago many times over and recently bought it again. This is the author's first book and he has written many more excellent sea stories too, but this one stands out for me; much in the way of the novel "The Cruel Sea"<br/><br/>HMS Ulysses is a frigate which is part of the arctic convoy runs during World War II to Murmansk. These convoy runs were fraught with constant danger from the atrocious weather conditions at sea but also from the risk of destruction</span>
  <span id="freeText12031294983686450618" style="display:none">I first read this brilliant heart-stopping book years ago many times over and recently bought it again. This is the author's first book and he has written many more excellent sea stories too, but this one stands out for me; much in the way of the novel "The Cruel Sea"<br /><br />HMS Ulysses is a frigate which is part of the arctic convoy runs during World War II to Murmansk. These convoy runs were fraught with constant danger from the atrocious weather conditions at sea but also from the risk of destruction by Germany’s U-Boats. The dreadful daily conditions are appalling for the men who suffer aboard her and they are physically and emotionally stretched far beyond their human limits. His dramatic descriptions are very powerful. The characters are true to life in conditions where human beings are asked to exhibit personal emotional strength and bravery, a readiness to face everything thrown at them in the most terrifying and heart-stopping conditions with a unity that overcomes all that face them every day and every night too. Suspense fills every drama-filled page. It is an incredibly moving record of the horrors of war, not the glory.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12031294983686450618" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2666227552" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2666227552" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2666227552?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=british-isles">british-isles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2666227552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11614120153324552121"><i>HMS Ulysses</i> was Alister MacLean's first novel, later he would be famous for such tales as <i> Where Eagles Dare</i>, <i>The Guns of Navarone</i>, and various others, but anyhow before all that after a stint in the Royal Navy during the war he became a school teacher and was labouring away at that trade when he entered a short story writing competition with the then very considerable prize of One hundred pounds. He not only won the money but was invited to write a book and <i>Ulysses</i> was the result in 1955.<br/><br/>The no</span>
  <span id="freeText11614120153324552121" style="display:none"><i>HMS Ulysses</i> was Alister MacLean's first novel, later he would be famous for such tales as <i> Where Eagles Dare</i>, <i>The Guns of Navarone</i>, and various others, but anyhow before all that after a stint in the Royal Navy during the war he became a school teacher and was labouring away at that trade when he entered a short story writing competition with the then very considerable prize of One hundred pounds. He not only won the money but was invited to write a book and <i>Ulysses</i> was the result in 1955.<br /><br />The novel tells the story of the flag ship escorting an Arctic convoy to Murmansk during the second world war. We are told that this ship is pretty exceptional, but it becomes clear that for all its engine power and armaments that it is effectively a set of potential death traps riveted together and capable of floating. No spoiler as we are told this almost immediately - we know from the start that there is a war on, also that the Arctic in winter is exceptionally cold, that the crew are exhausted having been escorting convoys for so long that there are more or less dead on their feet, indeed they are so weakened by chronic lack of sleep and living off corned beef sandwiches that TB is rampant. As maybe you have guessed MacLean was aiming for intensity of atmosphere.<br /><br />As a novel, it does not break the ice, there is hardly any characterisation, nor is there much plot. And there can't be much of either. MacLean short cuts by telling us what his characters are like, and concentrates on intensity of atmosphere and incident. While in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/183586.The_Cruel_Sea" title="The Cruel Sea by Nicholas Monsarrat" rel="nofollow noopener">The Cruel Sea</a> we follow a crew through most of the war (or over what feels like it), this novel runs over only a few days, but it is action packed like a Jackie Chan film - there is always something going on (or going wrong).<br /><br /><i>Cruel Sea</i> was written a few years before <i>Ulysses</i> and I could see the latter book, in the finest tradition of the service, as effectively saying 'you think they had it tough in your book, well you ain't seen anything yet!'. At first I felt that <i>Ulysses</i> was the better of the two books the same faults but a tense and compelling read, now that I've finished I am quite content to let them sink to the sea bed. It is exactly like an action film, ie a sugar rush of things happening quickly (in this case as quickly as the slowest vessel in the convoy).<br /><br />I felt while reading that the novel says something about 1950s Britain, some rationing still going on, the sense of enduring hardship, you just have to keep slogging on, keep your faith in Harold Macmillen and maybe after twenty years you might get a council house with a bathroom and an indoor toilet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11614120153324552121" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17255671051224045164">I must have been about 14 when I first read this book and I can remember being blown away by it; it was the best book that I had ever read. I recently found a copy in a second hand bookshop and it was with some trepidation that I began to reread it, afraid that it would not live up to my expectations. I am glad to report that I needed not to worry; the novel was all that I remembered it as being. It is, along with The Cruel Sea and The Cain Mutiny, one of the best explorations of naval warfare i</span>
  <span id="freeText17255671051224045164" style="display:none">I must have been about 14 when I first read this book and I can remember being blown away by it; it was the best book that I had ever read. I recently found a copy in a second hand bookshop and it was with some trepidation that I began to reread it, afraid that it would not live up to my expectations. I am glad to report that I needed not to worry; the novel was all that I remembered it as being. It is, along with The Cruel Sea and The Cain Mutiny, one of the best explorations of naval warfare in WW11, focusing not only on the battles but also on the effects of the cold, the fear and the danger on the men who sailed in those ships. My uncle was on a ship sunk on an Atlantic convoy and although he was picked up, more dead than alive, he never recovered from the experience. So, I suppose this and books like The Cruel Sea have a special resonance for me. </span>

<div id="review_1138871884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1138871884" />
    <a title="A.L. Sowards" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2048362-a-l-sowards"><img alt="A.L. Sowards" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603467220p2/2048362.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1138871884?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="A.L. Sowards" class="user" itemprop="url" name="A.L. Sowards" href="/user/show/2048362-a-l-sowards">A.L. Sowards</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2048362-a-l-sowards?shelf=wwii-fiction">wwii-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2048362-a-l-sowards?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2048362-a-l-sowards?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2048362-a-l-sowards?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1138871884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6591683515646326017">I was excited to read this book, because I used to love Alistair MacLean. But I was also nervous to read this book, because I used to love Alistair MacLean, and what if he wasn’t as good as I remembered? I did enjoy the book, but it took me a while to get into it. I think that’s because the protagonist is the crew as a whole, so it didn’t feel like there was a main character, just a lot of secondary characters. But as with most novels, by the middle, even secondary characters can be compelling a</span>
  <span id="freeText6591683515646326017" style="display:none">I was excited to read this book, because I used to love Alistair MacLean. But I was also nervous to read this book, because I used to love Alistair MacLean, and what if he wasn’t as good as I remembered? I did enjoy the book, but it took me a while to get into it. I think that’s because the protagonist is the crew as a whole, so it didn’t feel like there was a main character, just a lot of secondary characters. But as with most novels, by the middle, even secondary characters can be compelling and I may have shed a few tears by the end of the story. <br />	<br />The book revolves around the WWII Arctic convoys. The <i>Ulysses</i> had been on Arctic duty for some time as the book begins, and the duty isn’t easy. It’s so rough that they’ve just squashed a slight mutiny. Despite their exhaustion and bad morale, they set out again to escort ships carrying fuel, tanks, and planes to the Soviet Union. Along they way they battle horrid weather, the German navy (surface and underwater fleet), the German air force, and indifference and incompetence from the Royal Navy back in England. They rarely have more than a minute or two of down time before the next crisis emerges, so it’s a good novel for fans of fast-paced, action-packed stories. Many of his other novels have happier endings, so if you prefer happy endings over beautifully tragic books, I’d recommend trying a different one. On the other hand, this one does a good job giving readers a taste of the trials and sacrifices common on assignments like that, giving me a greater appreciation for what WWII Arctic convoys went through. It’s fiction, but I doubt it’s too far off reality. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6591683515646326017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1824440085197500394" style="display:none">Alistair Maclean was on to a winner with his first novel HMS Ulysses portraying the plight of British Navy in the harsh winter conditions during Arctic Convoys which were under constant danger of German U Boats and Warships.<br />Maclean's writing is so engrossing that you feel that you are there with the crew. <span id="freeTextContainer18009801911147231265">HMS Ulysses is a ship badly in need of rest., Having already been on several Arctic runs to Murmansk, she and her borderline mutinous crew are being sent on another high-speed convoy with supplies desperately needed by the Russians.   <span id="freeText18009801911147231265" style="display:none">HMS Ulysses is a ship badly in need of rest., Having already been on several Arctic runs to Murmansk, she and her borderline mutinous crew are being sent on another high-speed convoy with supplies desperately needed by the Russians. It’s FR 77; the weather is deteriorating, and the Nazis know the convoy is on its way. No rescue ships on this convoy; given what happened to the Stockport and Zafaaran, (both torpedoed with a loss of all hands and many who had been rescued from other ships that had been sunk) it probably wouldn’t make any difference anyway. <br /><br />MacLean’s nautical descriptions excel at vividness. And the Ulysses must fight its way through one of the worst storms ever recorded and no one understands or describes a warship moving through such an event as MacLean. <i><br /><br />The cold was now intense: ice formed in cabins and mess-decks: fresh-water systems froze solid: metal contracted, hatch-covers jammed, door hinges locked in frozen immobility, the oil in the searchlight controls gummed up and made them useless. To keep a watch, especially a watch on the bridge was torture: the first shock of that bitter wind seared the lungs, left a man fighting for breath. . .But the real danger of the ice lay in its weight. A ship, to use technical terms, can be either stiff or tender. If she's stiff, she has a low centre of gravity, rolls easily, but whips back quickly and is extremely stable and safe. If she's tender, with a high centre of gravity, she rolls reluctantly but comes back even more reluctantly, is unstable and unsafe. And if a ship were tender, and hundreds of tons of ice. . ..</i> And then there were the torpedoes...<br /><br /><i>“"The sea was on fire. Flat, calm, burdened with hundreds of tons of fuel oil, it was a vast carpet of licking, twisting flames. That much, for a second, and that only, Vallery saw: then with heartstopping shock, with physically sickening abruptness, he saw something else again: the burning sea was alive with swimming, struggling men. Not a handful, not even dozens, but literally hundreds, soundlessly screaming, agonizingly dying in the barbarous contrariety of drowning and cremation. <br /><br />"For a man in the sea, oil is an evil thing. It clogs his movements, burns his eyes, sears his lungs and tears away his stomach in uncontrollable paroxysms of retching; but oil on fire is a hellish thing, death by torture, a slow, shrieking death by drowning, by burning, by asphyxiation-for the flames devour all the life-giving oxygen on the surface of the sea. And not even in the bitter Arctic is there the merciful extinction by cold, for the insulation of an oil-soaked body stretches a dying man on the rack for eternity, carefully prfeserves him for the last excruciating refinement of agony.”</i><br /><br />This is not a feel-good book. It’s about as realistic a portrayal of the Murmansk run in mid-winter as one could imagine. Characters you like die. War sucks. The Murmansk run was a killer. It’s based, in part, on the experiences of convoy PQ17. Ripping good story. One Amazon reviewer noted, “Don't read this book unless you plan on leaving a part of yourself in the frigid waters of the North Atlantic.” Impossible to say it better.<br /><br />MacLean knows whereof he speaks. He saw service in the Royal Navy in the Arctic, Mediterranean and Far East theaters and was, in fact, involved in naval action against the Tirpitz while serving in the Arctic. I believe Ulysses was his first book, appearing in 1955, followed by The Guns of Navarone.<br /><br />Monsarrat’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/183586.The_Cruel_Sea" title="The Cruel Sea by Nicholas Monsarrat" rel="nofollow noopener">The Cruel Sea</a> is perhaps slightly better if less depressing. Another favorite is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1510289.The_Captain" title="The Captain by Jan de Hartog" rel="nofollow noopener">The Captain</a> by Jan de Hartog (different in tone from MacLean as de Hartog was a pacifist.) A really good non-fiction account is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2577763.Arctic_Convoys_1941_1945" title="Arctic Convoys 1941-1945 by Richard Woodman" rel="nofollow noopener">Arctic Convoys 1941-1945</a> by Richard Woodman. One I just added to my TBR list is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13026671.Arctic_Convoy_Pq8_The_Story_Of_Capt_Robert_Brundle_And_The_Ss_Harmatris" title="Arctic Convoy Pq8 The Story Of Capt Robert Brundle And The Ss Harmatris by Michael Wadsworth" rel="nofollow noopener">Arctic Convoy Pq8: The Story Of Capt Robert Brundle And The Ss Harmatris</a>. A view from the commons sailor side is COXSWAIN IN THE NORTHERN CONVOYS which has been republished on the Internet at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.naval-history.net/WW2Memoir-RussianConvoyCoxswain.htm">http://www.naval-history.net/WW2Memoi...</a>. You can reformat it at readability.com and then send it to your Kindle.<br /><br />References related to Murmansk Run: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murmansk_Run">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Murmansk...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="18009801911147231265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer895089501" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12080194142224571620">This was Alistair MacLean's first book, and surprisingly (IMHO) also his best. With MacLean's easy understanding of the military and its technology/jargon, as well as his well-justified "a star is born" reviews, <b>HMS Ulysses</b> was probably the <b>Hunt for Red October</b> of it's day. It's lack the complex plotting of his later thrillers for which he is more famous, and doesn't have one easily identifiable lead character. In total, it's really much closer to Michener's <b>The Bridges at Toki-Ri</b> (which perhaps</span>
  <span id="freeText12080194142224571620" style="display:none">This was Alistair MacLean's first book, and surprisingly (IMHO) also his best. With MacLean's easy understanding of the military and its technology/jargon, as well as his well-justified "a star is born" reviews, <b>HMS Ulysses</b> was probably the <b>Hunt for Red October</b> of it's day. It's lack the complex plotting of his later thrillers for which he is more famous, and doesn't have one easily identifiable lead character. In total, it's really much closer to Michener's <b>The Bridges at Toki-Ri</b> (which perhaps not coincidentally, was published just two years before <b>Ulysses</b>) than to any of MacLean's other work - and like <b>Bridges</b>, it paints a realistic picture of the randomness and senselessness of war. <br /><br />Many of MacLean's other books are good-to-sometimes-great yarns and a lot of fun; but with just a few exceptions they are read, enjoyed and then forgotten. But <b>Ulysses</b> is in a whole different league. Unremittingly brutal but beautifully written and deeply felt, this book truly deserves its status as a classic.</span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1155416160" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1155416160" />
    <a title="Daniel Villines" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2266055-daniel-villines"><img alt="Daniel Villines" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536179787p2/2266055.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1155416160?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Villines" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Villines" href="/user/show/2266055-daniel-villines">Daniel Villines</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1155416160" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3078560378471020665">It’s revealing when a review starts out with a reference to a different book. In fact, <i>The Cruel Sea</i> by Nicholas Monsarrat remains my favorite novel about life in the Royal Navy during the years that comprised World War II. <i>HMS Ulysses</i> has many of the same elements as Monsarrat’s novel, but Monsarrat practices control over his writing, he knows that magical point where language needs to stop so that the reader’s imagination can complete the imagery and feelings being conveyed. In <i>Ulysses</i>, Alista</span>
  <span id="freeText3078560378471020665" style="display:none">It’s revealing when a review starts out with a reference to a different book. In fact, <i>The Cruel Sea</i> by Nicholas Monsarrat remains my favorite novel about life in the Royal Navy during the years that comprised World War II. <i>HMS Ulysses</i> has many of the same elements as Monsarrat’s novel, but Monsarrat practices control over his writing, he knows that magical point where language needs to stop so that the reader’s imagination can complete the imagery and feelings being conveyed. In <i>Ulysses</i>, Alistair MacLean tends to monopolize the reader’s mind. It’s certainly not intolerable, but he does over-describe things to the point where it feels like one’s imagination has been pushed aside.<br /><br />The action in <i>Ulysses</i> is a bit over the top; as if MacLean is trying to compress the entire variety of wartime experiences into the single mission being chronicled. At times, the action is gripping, but in total it becomes overwhelming to the point of disbelief. For those that enjoy Westerns that feature forever-loaded revolvers and heros that escape death time after time, this story of survival is the naval equivalent of that genre. <br /><br />As war stories often go, the characters tend to be instruments of the plot. Bad things happen to them, but the feelings of the victims cannot be fully realized because much of their background is missing. To make matters worse, the characters are referred to at different times by one of their many monikers. Their rank, position, the acronym of their position, their last name, nickname, and first name are all used interchangeably, which creates confusion and further distances the reader from the characters.<br /><br />Overall, <i>HMS Ulysses</i> is not a bad naval war novel. As a first novel, MacLean makes an honest attempt to convey and expand upon his own first-hand experiences aboard the HMS Royalist during WWII. The sincerity comes through and I felt that he was reaching for something greater than his story. The problem with <i>HMS Ulysses</i> may very well be that MacLean reached too far.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3078560378471020665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_3477011977" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3477011977" />
    <a title="Steve Birchmore" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12993562-steve-birchmore"><img alt="Steve Birchmore" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348136956p2/12993562.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3477011977?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve Birchmore" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve Birchmore" href="/user/show/12993562-steve-birchmore">Steve Birchmore</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12993562-steve-birchmore?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12993562-steve-birchmore?shelf=british-fiction">british-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3477011977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15749767533447162763">A grim tale of heroism on the Murmansk convoys in WWII. <br/><br/>I don't know if this book is generally considered an anti-war novel, but I think it should be if it isn't. I'm not sure if I can say I really enjoyed it, but it certainly is compelling. <br/><br/>There is a scene in the film 'The Cruel Sea' where the corvette's ASDIC operator locates a U-boat that has just sunk a ship in the convoy. The trouble is, the U-boat seems to be right underneath where survivors of the sunken ship are in the sea. The commande</span>
  <span id="freeText15749767533447162763" style="display:none">A grim tale of heroism on the Murmansk convoys in WWII. <br /><br />I don't know if this book is generally considered an anti-war novel, but I think it should be if it isn't. I'm not sure if I can say I really enjoyed it, but it certainly is compelling. <br /><br />There is a scene in the film 'The Cruel Sea' where the corvette's ASDIC operator locates a U-boat that has just sunk a ship in the convoy. The trouble is, the U-boat seems to be right underneath where survivors of the sunken ship are in the sea. The commander of the corvette , played by Jack Hawkins, realises he has to fire depth charges into the water where the sunken ship survivors are, killing them, in order to sink the U-boat as it may sink more ships, and kill more men. I understand this scene is used in philosophy courses to illustrate the 'trolley problem' where one must choose that some must die in order to save more. They fire the depth charges, the survivors are killed, if I remember rightly, the men on the corvette's face look up as the depth charges explode, as if watching the survivors remains flying into the air. They then lose ASDIC contact. Perhaps it was a mistake. Perhaps the U-boat was never there. One of the sailors on the corvette shouts "bloody murderer!" at Jack Hawkins who has a look of horror on his face. Nobody there knows what Jack Hawkins knows, what lead him to the decision to depth charge the struggling survivors in the sea. The ASDIC operator doesn't know there are survivors above where he said the U-Boat is, the sailors manning the guns on the little corvette don't know Jack Hawkins has been told there is a U-Boat underneath the survivors. The survivors don't know why the corvette is sailing straight for them at full speed, and then, firing depth charges at them. Only Jack Hawkins has the crucial information. It looks like he deliberately killed those desperate men rather than take them on board his litttle ship. Well, HMS Ulysses is like that scene, but over and over again. Its horrible.<br /><br />I've read quite a few war memoirs and war novels, but I think this might be the grimmest.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15749767533447162763" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_472325977" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/472325977" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/335708-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228259480p2/335708.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/472325977?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/335708-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer472325977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12540154767295992685">The author describes the H.M.S. Ulysses as "The complete, the perfect fighting machine, man's ultimate, so far, in his attempt to weld science and savagery into an instrument of destruction. The perfect fighting machine---.........". Impressive, but its reputation has been unjustifiably sullied. The story places the reader on board as an observer in this intense telling of a naval story out of World War II. It can be a stressful read. So much so that the reader must look away from time to time a</span>
  <span id="freeText12540154767295992685" style="display:none">The author describes the H.M.S. Ulysses as "The complete, the perfect fighting machine, man's ultimate, so far, in his attempt to weld science and savagery into an instrument of destruction. The perfect fighting machine---.........". Impressive, but its reputation has been unjustifiably sullied. The story places the reader on board as an observer in this intense telling of a naval story out of World War II. It can be a stressful read. So much so that the reader must look away from time to time and take a deep relaxing breath. The reader follows along as the crew faces: The cruelty of an arctic winter, death and privation, destruction, unrelenting bad weather, blizzards, the worst storm of the war, the roughest seas imaginable, ocean warfare, U-boats, air attacks, constant tension, no relief, one mission after another without letup, numbing intense cold, being stretched past their limits, physical exhaustion, weakness, hunger, snow, ice, hurricane force winds, 24 hr nights, miserable dawns, ice everywhere, chaos, unimaginable fatigue, and all without end. I am late arriving to the party as a reader of this 1955 book. If not better, it is certainly on a par with MacClean's "Guns of Navarone. Thr author writes with authority and his verbal imagery is extraordinary. The reader should come away with an appreciation of the challenges our parents, grandparents, and great grandparents had to face during WWII. So could you have done as well? If you think not, then you had better re-boot or reconfigure. They are us and we are them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12540154767295992685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10144607877122160029">(From April 2012, previously posted on Amazon)<br/><br/>One of the greatest books ever written.<br/><br/>You may think that's an exaggeration but for me, having read a lot of books of all kinds over the years, it's true. Have you ever read a paragraph, midway through a book, that made you stop? Just stop. Then read it again. And then once more, just because it completely takes your breath away. I never had until I read "HMS Ulysses".<br/><br/>I had never read any Alistair MacLean even though many of his titles were familiar </span>
  <span id="freeText10144607877122160029" style="display:none">(From April 2012, previously posted on Amazon)<br /><br />One of the greatest books ever written.<br /><br />You may think that's an exaggeration but for me, having read a lot of books of all kinds over the years, it's true. Have you ever read a paragraph, midway through a book, that made you stop? Just stop. Then read it again. And then once more, just because it completely takes your breath away. I never had until I read "HMS Ulysses".<br /><br />I had never read any Alistair MacLean even though many of his titles were familiar from films. Reader reviews steered me to this book and I will always be glad they did. Rarely can a writer transport you to a time, place and environment you can't possibly imagine, and immerse you in it so fully.<br /><br />I stand by my title for this review. If you are not convinced, find the passage, "At 2230 Ulysses crossed the Arctic Circle. The monster struck.", then read on for the following page and tell me you don't stop reading and just stare, open-mouthed and with goose bumps for at least a minute or two.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10144607877122160029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19389276-aaron-krebs?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1496455151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4669685421548453873">I worried that the years might have aged this book a bit, but happily that was not the case.<br/>July 2016 reread<br/><br/>This is the only book I can recall dealing with life or adventure on the high seas that I have cared much for, and it turns out it's probably my favorite book, period.<br/><br/>It is the tale of a light cruiser on an arctic convoy to Russia - a punishment issued to an already fatigued crew by an infuriatingly cold and punitive Admiralty for mutinous actions on their prior convoy run. MacLean subsequ</span>
  <span id="freeText4669685421548453873" style="display:none">I worried that the years might have aged this book a bit, but happily that was not the case.<br />July 2016 reread<br /><br />This is the only book I can recall dealing with life or adventure on the high seas that I have cared much for, and it turns out it's probably my favorite book, period.<br /><br />It is the tale of a light cruiser on an arctic convoy to Russia - a punishment issued to an already fatigued crew by an infuriatingly cold and punitive Admiralty for mutinous actions on their prior convoy run. MacLean subsequently wrote dozens of action adventure novels, many made into famous movies, but this is far and away my favorite of his stories - though fictional it nearly qualifies as historical, given MacLean's own experiences in the Royal Navy.<br /><br />I'm a sucker for tales of loyalty and sacrifice and the ordeals of the convoy to which the Ulysses is assigned provide non-stop examples of both. It's almost 60 years old but still feels fresh.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4669685421548453873" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer204836860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14765149140018576648">I'm left speechless by this story. A truly amazing story of heroism of men tired beyond belief fighting a war in conditions unbelievable but true. The HMS Ulysses is a Royal Navy cruiser whose crew have recently mutinied and are tasked once again to meet and escort a convoy on the Murmansk run to Russia. The crew is beaten, tired and the Captain is dying. They sail to meet the convoy of merchant ships at Iceland to take over from the warships escorting the convoy from Canada. The result is a fas</span>
  <span id="freeText14765149140018576648" style="display:none">I'm left speechless by this story. A truly amazing story of heroism of men tired beyond belief fighting a war in conditions unbelievable but true. The HMS Ulysses is a Royal Navy cruiser whose crew have recently mutinied and are tasked once again to meet and escort a convoy on the Murmansk run to Russia. The crew is beaten, tired and the Captain is dying. They sail to meet the convoy of merchant ships at Iceland to take over from the warships escorting the convoy from Canada. The result is a fascinating, horrifying, touching story of this voyage; the love of the crew for their ill Captain, his love for them; the many personalities of the crew and the ordeal they must sail through. The story makes me think of my father as he also sailed to Murmansk, something he doesn't tell me much about. I've read this story before, but so long ago. I'm glad I read again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14765149140018576648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11327565091563112575">Alistair MacLean’s “H.M.S. Ulysses” is an entertaining tale of the horrors of the Arctic convoys to Russia during World War II. It is, just that, entertaining -- while sometimes lapsing into “soap opera.” A back cover blurb states “H.M.S. Ulysses is in the same class as The Cruel Sea.” I disagree. MacLean’s novel is more akin to a Douglas Reeman book, but while Reeman concentrates on action, MacLean concentrates on characters. Neither, as an author of nautical action tales, is in a class with Ni</span>
  <span id="freeText11327565091563112575" style="display:none">Alistair MacLean’s “H.M.S. Ulysses” is an entertaining tale of the horrors of the Arctic convoys to Russia during World War II. It is, just that, entertaining -- while sometimes lapsing into “soap opera.” A back cover blurb states “H.M.S. Ulysses is in the same class as The Cruel Sea.” I disagree. MacLean’s novel is more akin to a Douglas Reeman book, but while Reeman concentrates on action, MacLean concentrates on characters. Neither, as an author of nautical action tales, is in a class with Nicholas Monsarrat. Alistair MacLean’s novel is worthy of a weak Three Stars. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1751336105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10257751593042047620">Astounding how much this book reminded me of Douglas Reeman--one of the latter's weaker efforts. Too drawn out, too psychological; too many "black hat" characters. But as where Reeman always integrated those elements in the plot, many here were tacked on as afterthoughts--e.g., the Rating who sacrifices himself for the ship to atone for a murder that took place before the book starts! And Reeman never killed off so many "white hats."<br/><br/>Still, MacLean knows boats, and served in the "wavy Navy" durin</span>
  <span id="freeText10257751593042047620" style="display:none">Astounding how much this book reminded me of Douglas Reeman--one of the latter's weaker efforts. Too drawn out, too psychological; too many "black hat" characters. But as where Reeman always integrated those elements in the plot, many here were tacked on as afterthoughts--e.g., the Rating who sacrifices himself for the ship to atone for a murder that took place before the book starts! And Reeman never killed off so many "white hats."<br /><br />Still, MacLean knows boats, and served in the "wavy Navy" during WW2, including on the deadly Arctic supply run to Archangel, Russia. Hell on the high seas.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10257751593042047620" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3479162759" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3479162759" />
    <a title="Benjamin Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4209082-benjamin-thomas"><img alt="Benjamin Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451660582p2/4209082.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3479162759?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benjamin Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benjamin Thomas" href="/user/show/4209082-benjamin-thomas">Benjamin Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/22450723-h-m-s-ulysses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4209082-benjamin-thomas?shelf=thriller-military">thriller-military</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4209082-benjamin-thomas?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4209082-benjamin-thomas?shelf=nautical-fiction">nautical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3479162759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18351559243657529353">From the year 1941 until the end of World War II, Alistair Maclean served in the Royal Navy with the ranks of Ordinary Seaman, Able Seaman, and Leading Torpedo Operator. This experience served him well when he launched his writing career with his debut novel, “H.M.S. Ulysses” in 1955, considered by many to be his best. Of course, he went on to great acclaim and a distinguished bestseller career despite the fact that he claimed never to have enjoyed writing but did it merely as a source of income</span>
  <span id="freeText18351559243657529353" style="display:none">From the year 1941 until the end of World War II, Alistair Maclean served in the Royal Navy with the ranks of Ordinary Seaman, Able Seaman, and Leading Torpedo Operator. This experience served him well when he launched his writing career with his debut novel, “H.M.S. Ulysses” in 1955, considered by many to be his best. Of course, he went on to great acclaim and a distinguished bestseller career despite the fact that he claimed never to have enjoyed writing but did it merely as a source of income.<br /><br />This novel features the HMS Ulysses, a light cruiser that is among the best armed and fastest ships in the Navy. The story begins in the aftermath of a mutiny born of an overworked and exhausted crew. Senior Navy officers have decided to let her prove herself by sending her out on yet another mission: to escort a critical convoy of 32 ships through the treacherous Arctic Sea to Russian allies in Murmansk. Along the way, they face extreme challenges in the form of unusually harsh Arctic storms, German ships, packs of U-boats, as well as repeated air attacks. <br /><br />A number of interesting characters populate HMS Ulysses, not the least of which is her Captain, a man suffering the advanced stages of TB but who nevertheless symbolizes the good luck of the ship’s past missions and any hope to survive this one. Few crew members are motivated by concepts such as honor, courage, etc. but rather it is their resilience that pushes them to hero status. There is a fair amount of technical jargon, but MacLean’s prose is filled with vivid descriptions of the harsh environment, the raw feeling of utter loneliness, and the crew’s growing exhaustion and desperation. This is a marvelous depiction of wartime naval operations, and a stunning portrayal of how men can push past the bounds of endurance.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18351559243657529353" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Brutal and relentless, the description of the borderline crew fighting the enemy as well as the elements during an Arctic convoy is both thrilling and exhausting and had MacLean continued upon this path, he would be remembered differently. Now, he did give us some good thrillers, such as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/296298.The_Guns_of_Navarone' title='The Guns of Navarone by Alistair MacLean' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Guns of Navarone</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/148337.Where_Eagles_Dare_' title='Where Eagles Dare  by Alistair MacLean' rel='nofollow noopener'>Where Eagles Dare</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/149610.Night_Without_End' title='Night Without End by Alistair MacLean' rel='nofollow noopener'>Night Without </a></span>
  <span id="freeText693765127616174965" style="display:none">MacLean's first book drew from his real-life experiences on a war ship during world war II. Brutal and relentless, the description of the borderline crew fighting the enemy as well as the elements during an Arctic convoy is both thrilling and exhausting and had MacLean continued upon this path, he would be remembered differently. <div id="review_235490726" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/235490726" />
    <a title="heidi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19026-heidi"><img alt="heidi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1302740285p2/19026.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/235490726?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="heidi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="heidi" href="/user/show/19026-heidi">heidi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19026-heidi?shelf=paper">paper</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer235490726" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16334980023097245768">The thing I love about Powell's books is that you can ask the much-pierced 20-something at the desk about the location of bestsellers of the 60s and 70s, and they will answer you without so much as a pause for thought.<br/><br/>Anyway, Powell's: a great source for the pop-action-stories of bygone decades.<br/><br/>I picked up an omnibus of full-length novels. I already own /Where Eagles Dare/ and /When Eight Bells Toll/, but can you believe I did not actually own /Ice Station Zebra/ or /The Guns of Navarone/. But I</span>
  <span id="freeText16334980023097245768" style="display:none">The thing I love about Powell's books is that you can ask the much-pierced 20-something at the desk about the location of bestsellers of the 60s and 70s, and they will answer you without so much as a pause for thought.<br /><br />Anyway, Powell's: a great source for the pop-action-stories of bygone decades.<br /><br />I picked up an omnibus of full-length novels. I already own /Where Eagles Dare/ and /When Eight Bells Toll/, but can you believe I did not actually own /Ice Station Zebra/ or /The Guns of Navarone/. But I have them all now, as well as HMS Ulysses, which I had never read before. I am going to tell you all sorts of plot points, on the theory that this book is 20 years older than I am.<br /><br />We start with an abortive mutiny. The poor sods crewing this escort ship are officially about to have a very bad trip. They are slated to do yet another run of the convoy to Murmansk, which is pretty much Dead Men Sailing In Ships. They are pretty sure that they do not want to do this again. However, the ship is sent out again, under the auspices of the kindly, overworked doctor, the kindly, stoic captain, and the kindly admiral. The whole book could really be titled "HMS Ulysses and the Terrible, Horrible, No-Good, Very Bad Convoy". In only a rough semblance of order, here are SOME of the things that happened.<br /><br />* U-boats<br />* More U-boats<br />* Henkels<br />* Being bombed<br />* Being torpedoed <br />* Catastrophic winter hurricane<br />* Rogue waves<br />* Stukkas<br />* People having to be trapped on the wrong side of a flood door to Save the Ship (x2)<br />* Tall taciturn strong man sacrifices self in misguided expiation of sins<br />* Captain dies of TB<br />* Betrayal by the command structure<br />* Poor bastard finds out his sisters and mother died in a bombing the day they ship. Then his brother is killed. Then he has to fire on his father's ship because it was endangering the convoy. <br />* Admiral goes crazy<br />* Admiral dies of frostbite/amputation<br />* Bad apple saves the engineer who gave him a chance<br /><br />Really, it was like a microcosm of horsemen, what with the war, pestilence (TB), starvation, and death. By halfway through the book I was cackling madly and reading choice excerpts to my roommate. It was just the sort of adventure book I love, full of hard luck and people pulling through anyway.<br /><br />Amazingly, some few battered survivors lived to tell the story.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16334980023097245768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1702473549?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sherry McGhay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sherry McGhay" href="/user/show/7094091-sherry-mcghay">Sherry McGhay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7094091-sherry-mcghay?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7094091-sherry-mcghay?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1702473549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11224149408672093614">Well, it took me two months to get through this one...that should tell you that it wasn't one of my favorites. Why didn't I just give up? My friend has been trying to get me to read this book for years. His daughter has been telling me not to waste my time because it's a horrible book. My summer reading goal was to read the book and decide for myself. My vote would fall somewhere in the middle. The story of the boat, it's battles, and the sailors was interesting enough to keep me plowing through</span>
  <span id="freeText11224149408672093614" style="display:none">Well, it took me two months to get through this one...that should tell you that it wasn't one of my favorites. Why didn't I just give up? My friend has been trying to get me to read this book for years. His daughter has been telling me not to waste my time because it's a horrible book. My summer reading goal was to read the book and decide for myself. My vote would fall somewhere in the middle. The story of the boat, it's battles, and the sailors was interesting enough to keep me plowing through the book. However, MacLean has an obsession for using nautical jargon, and I think he needs help. I can admit the fact that I know nothing about boats, beyond the fact that they are supposed to float. I tried to get past it...Googled some pictures of ships to try and educate myself about the nautical terms MacLean was using...learned a few of the basics. You need to have a fairly extensive nautical vocabulary to NOT get annoyed by MacLean's almost OCD use of so many specific nautical words. Then, on top of that, he likes to use lots of other big words. I'm a teacher/librarian, so I can appreciate that. However, since I was already looking up so many sailing terms, I didn't appreciate having to look up additional words. <br /><br />I loved the perseverance shown through the characters, their sacrifices, and personal stories. I wish MacLean would have reduced the number of characters and developed those left a little more deeply. They all seemed to blur together for me. At some point, MacLean should just take a lesson from Star Trek. In Star Trek, you know that characters dressed in a red uniform are not important to the story line and are probably going to die. If they're not important, then I don't need to know their name, rank, and useless background info.<br /><br />Is there an abridged version? I MIGHT be able to convincingly hand this to a teen reader if I knew some of the fluff was gone. I didn't hate it. The basic story line was compelling. I wanted to see if the Ulysses would make it to her final port. I was shocked and crushed when I found out why Ralston didn't want to torpedo one of their own ships that had been hit. I wanted to see how the captain would handle a possibly mutinous crew...the story was fine. I just found MacLean's writing style a bit verbose and annoying.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11224149408672093614" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1487309170?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shaunak Bhattacharya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shaunak Bhattacharya" href="/user/show/5216408-shaunak-bhattacharya">Shaunak Bhattacharya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1487309170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4834689885859713555">A bit tough to rate this book 4 stars..since there I went through lots of personal hurdle to read this,and at many points thought of giving it up because of countless descriptions about ships and their parts,which I had no idea about.<br/><br/>Still, it was Maclean's writing which just dragged me slowly into the book and it were the descriptions of the ACTUAL war and the action which just blew me away. Its NOT a very light book as I guessed. It required hell lot of concentration with some JAWDROPPING desc</span>
  <span id="freeText4834689885859713555" style="display:none">A bit tough to rate this book 4 stars..since there I went through lots of personal hurdle to read this,and at many points thought of giving it up because of countless descriptions about ships and their parts,which I had no idea about.<br /><br />Still, it was Maclean's writing which just dragged me slowly into the book and it were the descriptions of the ACTUAL war and the action which just blew me away. Its NOT a very light book as I guessed. It required hell lot of concentration with some JAWDROPPING descriptions...<br /><br />To quote from his own book at the end<br /><br />".....but it was a halting story,strangely lacking in conviction.The atmosphere,the surroundings were wrong_ the contrast between the peaceful warmth of these rooms and the inhuman cold and cruelty of the Arctic was an immense gulf that could be bridged only by experience and understanding"<br /><br />There....This one sentence brilliantly sums up the book....absolutely brilliantly...For a person like me who has not known War from a personal level, this book was a stunner with some terrific and yet horrific descriptions of Struggle,Heroism,and Courage that stood out over the individual characters like Vallery and Turner and the Kapok Kid and Nicholls and spread out over the entire crew in a unified battle for survival....<br /><br />Hats off Mr.Maclean.... </span>
  <a data-text-id="4834689885859713555" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_740111818" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/740111818" />
    <a title="Cphe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14907696-cphe"><img alt="Cphe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378006896p2/14907696.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/740111818?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cphe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cphe" href="/user/show/14907696-cphe">Cphe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14907696-cphe?shelf=absolutely-fabulous">absolutely-fabulous</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer740111818" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18061100401988925242">I would rate this higher if I could. There aren't, in my opinion enough superlatives to do this stirring Naval story justice. It's the story of the heroic H.M.S Ulysses and her crew who are ordered to undertake the escort of a convoy to Russia across the merciless and dangerous North Atlantic. For a crew that is at the end of its tether after months of battle, it's almost beyond belief.<br/><br/>It's a story of survival, for both the Ulysses and her crew against insurmountable odds. A story of men who hav</span>
  <span id="freeText18061100401988925242" style="display:none">I would rate this higher if I could. There aren't, in my opinion enough superlatives to do this stirring Naval story justice. It's the story of the heroic H.M.S Ulysses and her crew who are ordered to undertake the escort of a convoy to Russia across the merciless and dangerous North Atlantic. For a crew that is at the end of its tether after months of battle, it's almost beyond belief.<br /><br />It's a story of survival, for both the Ulysses and her crew against insurmountable odds. A story of men who have been pushed beyond their mental, emotional and physical limits, who don't at first realise that they will be called upon to give even more.<br /><br />It's a journey fraught with difficulty, battle against enemy air attack, roving U-Boats harrying the convoy at every opportunity. Then there is the harsh and unrelenting environment, frostbite inducing cold, angry seas, freak waves, unrelenting storms, exhaustion.<br /><br />The writing is descriptive, you can almost feel yourself aboard the rolling, ice encrusted decks of the Ulysses. There is a wonderful cross section of characters who are well fleshed out. It's a stirring, heroic story that will stay in a reader's mind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18061100401988925242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LBhw+S55J6yQf5n16dKqtkc68DMA6tCWlilQlzfOSP4vQmwni//mYWVAQzz5IcqpMn+64wc+8gq3wYvjKSdEzQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('InuaUq6xAjn4ASnZSvq3ZQ/SB0NdcSdAp5k+Ls0PAbUhIYaMCzfD9A0+8xBaCdd6epdNk1qlBdyGceVa0+YNhg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3590376946771057474">Alistair Stuart MacLean (Scottish Gaelic: Alasdair MacGill-Eain), the son of a Scots Minister, was brought up in the Scottish Highlands. In 1941, at the age of eighteen, he joined the Royal Navy; two and a half years spent aboard a cruiser were to give him the background for <i>HMS Ulysses</i>, his first novel, the outstanding documentary novel on the war at sea. After the war he gained an English Honour</span>
  <span id="freeText3590376946771057474" style="display:none">Alistair Stuart MacLean (Scottish Gaelic: Alasdair MacGill-Eain), the son of a Scots Minister, was brought up in the Scottish Highlands. In 1941, at the age of eighteen, he joined the Royal Navy; two and a half years spent aboard a cruiser were to give him the background for <i>HMS Ulysses</i>, his first novel, the outstanding documentary novel on the war at sea. After the war he gained an English Honours degree at Glasgow University, and became a schoolmaster. In 1983, he was awarded a D. Litt. from the same university.<br /><br />Maclean is the author of twenty-nine world bestsellers and recognised as an outstanding writer in his own genre. Many of his titles have been adapted for film - <i>The Guns of the Navarone</i>, <i>The Satan Bug</i>, <i>Force Ten from Navarone</i>, <i>Where Eagles Dare</i> and <i>Bear Island</i> are among the most famous. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3590376946771057474" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;...the men of the Ulysses had no need to stand in shame...many had found, or were finding, that the point of no return was not necessarily the edge of the precipice: it could be the bottom of the valley, the beginning of the long climb up the far slope, and when a man had once begun that climb he never looked back to that other side.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/534042-the-men-of-the-ulysses-had-no-need-to-stand">13 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;She was still doing forty knots, driving in under the guns of the enemy, guns at maximum depression, when "A" magazine blew up, blasted off the entire bows in one shattering detonations. For a second, the lightened fo'c'sle reared high into the air" then it plunged down, deep down, into the shoulder of a rolling sea. She plunged down and kept on going down, driving down to the black floor of the Arctic, driven down by the madly spinning screws. The still thundering engines her own executioners.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1235818-she-was-still-doing-forty-knots-driving-in-under-the">5 likes</a>
